"Ugh, not again," you spout as the old wagon shutters, then dies. Stranded on the road again, but this time, your money has gone dry. All but your 50 dollar bill that Grandma gave you on her last Christmas. You've been saving it for when you needed it most, but surely, it won't be enough.

The door creaks open, and you step out into the night sky, coughing up the smoke from your fried vehicle. After pushing your car off the road and between the trees, there isn't much else left for you to do, so you begin to wander down the dark, lonely street.

But at the end of the road, where concrete turned to stone turned to dirt, you notice a light up ahead, on the top of a hill. 

As you waltz into the old, wooden shack, your eyes begin to light up with the fire of a thousand suns. Roulette wheels! Poker tables! And in a dark corner of the abandoned casino, sits a dealer, shuffling cards for a new round of Blackjack. That 50 dollars might just come in handy after all. Thanks, Grandma!

As you go to sit down at the table, you hear the Dealer cough, then watch as he sits up.

In a deep, and yet strained voice, the Dealer, cloaked in darkness, poses a question to you.

Would you like to play a game of Blackjack? You have $50

You get knocked over. You're covered in dog slobber. A golden retriever is standing on your chest.

You've never been happier.



As you're walking along the side of the road, something catches your eye. A flash of white, darting between the bushes. A rabbit!

Without thinking, you give chase. The little creature bounds ahead of you, zigging and zagging through the underbrush with seemingly effortless grace.

You run and run, but no matter how fast you go, the rabbit stays just out of reach. Its white tail bobs mockingly in the moonlight.

Finally, you stop, hands on your knees, gasping for breath. When you look up, the rabbit is gone, vanished into the night like it was never there.

You trudge back to your wagon, defeated. But something tells you this isn't the last time you'll see that rabbit.

...

Just a dream. Just a dream. Just a—

Just rain. You're fine. You're fine. You're fine.

He hands you a grimy business card through the crack in the window. It reads: "Grimy Gus's Pawn Emporium - We Buy What Others Won't"

"Come by sometime. You won't regret it. Probably."

He shuffles off into the morning mist before you can respond.

══════════ ~ ~ ~ Morning, Day 15 ~ ~ ~ ══════════
A bird is singing outside your car. You don't want to punch it. Progress.

You step out of your car in the casino parking lot and see yourself.

Not a mirror. Not a reflection. YOU. Standing about fifty feet away, staring back at you.

Same clothes. Same face. Same confused expression.

You blink. Your double blinks.

You take a step forward. It takes a step forward.

You wave. It waves.

And then it smiles-a smile you've never made, too wide, too knowing-and walks behind a van.

You run over. Nothing. No one. Just empty parking spaces.

You stand there for a long time, heart pounding, wondering if you're finally losing your mind.

Maybe you are.

You give Jameson directions to Tom's garage. The cowboy tips his hat.
"I owe you, friend. If Tom can help Thunder, I owe you everything."
Jameson leads Thunder away on foot. The horse nuzzles against his shoulder. Even sick, they trust each other completely.
You wish him luck. It's all you can afford right now.

══════════ ~ ~ ~ Morning, Day 28 ~ ~ ~ ══════════
You stretch out and feel every vertebra in your spine crack in the most satisfying way. Today is going to be good.

Your eyes open. At least, you think they do.
Your eyes are open. You can see the ceiling of your car. The streetlight outside casting its orange glow. Everything is normal.
Except you can't move.
Not paralyzed-numb. Paralyzed-trapped. Your body is awake but it won't listen. Your arms are dead weight. Your legs won't respond. You can't even turn your head.
Then you see it. In the corner of your vision. Something in the back seat.
A shape. Darker than dark. It doesn't have edges — it has suggestions of edges. Like smoke that decided to sit down.
It's watching you. You can feel it watching you even though it doesn't have eyes.
Your breathing gets faster. Your heart is hammering. But you. Can't. Move.
The shape leans forward. Closer. You can feel cold radiating off it like an open freezer.
Then — snap — your body releases. You bolt upright, gasping. The back seat is empty. Nothing there. Just the dark.
You don't sleep for the rest of the night. You sit with every light on and your eyes open, watching the back seat in the rearview mirror.
Nothing comes. But the cold lingers.

The performance begins. He mimes waking up in a car. Your car. He mimes playing cards. Winning. Losing. He mimes meeting different people—
Wait, is he doing Jameson? He's pretending to ride a horse. That's definitely Jameson.
Oh no. He's straightening an invisible tie. That's the red suit guy. Phil. He nails the judgmental stare.
The mime finishes with a new bit: he pulls an invisible rope and lifts himself off the ground, dangling in the air. Then he falls, catches himself, and takes a bow.
He's sweating under the face paint. This guy takes his craft seriously.
The mime beams. He reaches behind your ear and produces a very real, very shiny quarter.
Then another. And another. And another. This goes on for a while. By the time he stops, you've got $15 in quarters.

The trees outside your car window are too still. Unnaturally still.
You wade waist-deep through the swamp, the water cold and thick with silt. Every step is a struggle, and unseen things brush against your legs. The air is heavy with the scent of decay and blooming lilies. Fireflies blink in the darkness like tiny green stars.

A small wooden shack emerges from the fog, perched on stilts above the waterline. Candles flicker in the window, and hanging from the porch are bundles of dried herbs, animal bones, and what looks disturbingly like human teeth.

A woman's voice calls out: "I know you're out there, sugar. Come on up. The water ain't safe at night."

Do you approach the witch's shack?You climb the rickety ladder. The witch is ancient, her skin like dried leather, but her eyes are sharp and knowing. She's stirring a pot that smells like... chicken soup?

"Sit. Eat. Then we'll talk about what you owe me."

Do you eat her soup?The soup is delicious. Best thing you've eaten in weeks. The witch watches you with a crooked smile.

"Now, for payment. Give me something interesting, or I'll take something interesting."

She hands you a folded paper with strange symbols.

"You'll know when you need this. Maybe. If you're clever."

There won't be a next time. Will there?
You get back in your car. The calliope music plays for exactly three seconds from a radio station that doesn't exist. Then silence.

Waving the vehicle down, the limo slows, then stops before you. The door opens vertically, and a large red carpet is rolled out onto the street. You watch in awe as a man, with a combover and a tuxedo, walks out before you. He coughs, then speaks.
"Why hello there! The name's Oswald, as you can see by my nametag. Do you like my bowtie? Well of course you do! It appears your limousine has broken down." Oswald pulls a gold whistle out of his pocket, and blows into it deeply. "Oh Stuart!" You watch as a bald man in a tailcoat suit, no taller than 4 feet, hobbles over to Oswald's side.
"This is Stuart! He will fix your limousine up for a fair price. Let's say, I don't know, I suppose a fair price is 500,000 dollars. Okay, the look on your face says that I'm making a big mistake. Let's try $850, and Stuart here will get you back on the road! Do you accept?" 
"Really? You don't want my services? I'm so sorry Stuart, But it appears they don't want our services." Stuart begins to break down into tears, and he runs quickly back into the limo. "Shame on you! Shame on you! I hope to never see the likes of you again."
You watch as Oswald rolls up the red carpet, gets back in the limo, and drives off into the distance.

══════════ ~ ~ ~ Morning, Day 66 ~ ~ ~ ══════════
You stretch out and feel every vertebra in your spine crack in the most satisfying way. Today is going to be good.

You're driving when your car radio turns on. By itself.
You haven't touched it. The button is right there, untouched. But the dial is glowing and static is pouring through the speakers.
Not normal static. This static has a rhythm. A pulse. Like breathing.
You reach for the dial to turn it off. The moment your finger touches it, the static stops. Clear silence. Then:
"...eighty-seven... eighty-seven... eighty-seven..."
A voice. Robotic. Monotone. Repeating a number.
Then it changes:
"...fourteen... fourteen... fourteen..."
Then back to static. Then silence. The radio turns itself off.
You sit in your car, hand still on the dial, heart pounding. The numbers echo: 87, 14.
You scribble 87-14 on a receipt. You don't know why. Something tells you they mean something.
The radio stays off for the rest of the night. But the display flickers twice when you're not looking.

"The mime thing started as a disguise. Turns out, nobody looks twice at a mime. ""You're invisible. The perfect hiding spot is where everyone can see you."
He glances around nervously.
"I've been watching you because you're in their territory. The gambling road. ""They don't like people winning too much. Drawing attention."
Gerald nods approvingly.
"Smart. The less you know, the safer you are. Just... be careful."
He walks away. No moonwalk this time. Just a scared man in a brown jacket.

══════════ ~ ~ ~ Morning, Day 72 ~ ~ ~ ══════════
You actually want to get out of the car. This is a historic moment.

Through the windshield-again-a car is parked right in front of you. You can feel your blood start to boil. What's this guy's problem? As you open the door and get out of your car, you notice the man in his bright red suit, once again peering into your trunk.

The man sees you, and walks up to you, with a pistol holstered to his waist.

"You. I'm done playing around. It's time to move. I mean it." You look down at the gun on his waist. It looks fancy, and certainly deadly.

"I wouldn't test me if I were you. It's time to go, now."

"Will you leave?" "That's great. Fantastic. But I don't believe a word that comes out of your filthy mouth. Prove it. Leave. Go away. GET OUT."

You are fueled with anger. Who is this guy, and what gives him the right to harass you? All for being homeless? No longer. You reach for the gun on his waist.

You snatch the gun from his holster, and he tackles you to the ground. You fight and struggle, each of you with both hands on the pistol. In the distance, you hear the horn of a freight truck beginning to drive closer. The man punches you in the arm, and it stings. Without thinking twice, you give the man a headbutt, and he falls backwards into the road. You point the gun at the man, and he begins to cry.

"Please, I'm sorry. I didn't mean to cause any of this. ""I just, I hate seeing people living on the streets, all alone. ""I was just trying to help you. Just, please, for the love of god, don't hurt me."

As the man begs for his life, the freight truck continues to draw closer, and the horn gets louder. You point at the truck in the distance, but the man can't see through the tears in his eyes.

"Please, I have a family. I have children. My name is Phil. I don't wanna die. I'm too young. I can't die. I can't die. I ca-" You watch as the freight truck crushes Phil, and continues down the road. Nothing remained but the splotches of blood that splattered the road where he once stood.

After sitting a while, and recollecting your thoughts, you bring the pistol over to Phil's car, and throw it onto the passenger seat. Looking inside, the car has dice hanging on the mirror, and is filled to the brim with red suits. On the dashboard sits a photo of Phil, his wife, and his three kids, all wearing bright red suits. Phil might've been crazy, but at least he was consistent.

You get in the car, and drive it down the road, before turning into the woods. You drive a mile in, before parking the car before the lake. You get out, and push the car into the water, watching as it submerges.

When he's done, he reaches into his pocket and pulls out something small. A silver Dealer's Coin — worn smooth from years in his pocket.
"Sofia gave me this. Found it on the beach when she was eight. ""She said it was lucky. I don't believe in luck, but I believed in her."
"I want you to have it. Not because it's lucky. Because she would have wanted ""someone who plays at my table to carry it."
You take the coin. It's warm. Years of pocket warmth.
"Now sit down. Let's play cards. And this time, try to beat me for real."
Whiskers drops a dead bug on your chest to wake you up. Breakfast in bed, cat-style.
Having Whiskers by your side makes the world a little less cruel.

Sleep won't come. You try counting sheep. The sheep turn into cards. The cards turn into debts. The debts turn into a math problem you already know the answer to but keep solving anyway, hoping the answer changes.

You wonder if they know. How lucky the ordinary is. How you'd give everything you're chasing just to feel that ordinary again. Probably not. Nobody ever knows until it's already gone and they're lying in a car in the dark making deals with the ceiling.

Your body feels like it got a factory reset overnight. Joints? Smooth. Back? Fine. Mood? Immaculate.

The radio turns on again. 3:17 AM. You weren't sleeping — you never sleep well anymore — so you hear it clearly.
This time, the voice is different. Not robotic. Human. Someone reading from a script.
"Attention. This is a test of the Emergency Broadcast System. This is only a test."
Normal enough. Except it keeps going.
"The person in the car on Route 9 should know that we see them. We see the cards. We see the bets. We see the car with the bad transmission and the dog in the passenger seat."
"This is not a warning. This is a notification. You are being watched. For your own protection."
Click. Static. Silence.
Your hands are white-knuckled on the steering wheel. That broadcast described YOUR life. Your car. Your route. Your dog.
You spin the dial slowly. Station after station of normal radio — pop music, country, a preacher yelling about the end times (less scary than the broadcast). Nothing out of the ordinary.
Until you hit 87.14 FM.
87.14. The numbers from before. 87 and 14.
Dead air. Not static — silence. Like an open microphone in an empty room. You can hear ambient sound: a hum, a clock ticking, someone breathing.
Then: "Good. You found us."
The radio turns itself off.
Your mind feels... foggy. Something isn't right.

He vanishes into the shadows. You didn't see him go. He was just... gone.

══════════ ~ ~ ~ Morning, Day 84 ~ ~ ~ ══════════
With energy. Like, real energy. Not 'I didn't die in my sleep' energy.

You've been driving toward the signal. You don't know how you know which direction to go — the radio hums louder when you're heading the right way, softer when you're not.
It leads you off the main road, down a dirt path, past a rusted fence, to a clearing in the woods.
There's a tower. An old radio tower, fifty feet tall, barely standing. The metal is corroded, the guy wires are frayed, and the blinking red light at the top is the only sign it's still active.
At the base of the tower is a small concrete building. The door is steel, rusted, and slightly ajar.
You push the door open. It screams on its hinges.
Inside is a single room. Concrete walls, no windows. A desk with radio equipment — old equipment, vacuum tubes and dials, mixed with modern components that shouldn't be here.
A microphone sits on the desk. Next to it, a logbook.
You open the logbook. Pages and pages of entries. Dates, frequencies, and descriptions of... people. Drivers. Drifters. People on the road.
Your page is near the back. Your car's description. Your daily routine. Your gambling schedule. All documented in meticulous handwriting.
At the bottom of your entry:
"Subject shows resilience. Recommend continued observation. Do not intervene. Let the road decide."
The microphone crackles. A voice — the same voice from the broadcast — fills the room:
"You found the tower. Most people don't get this far. Now the question is: what are you going to do with what you've found?"
You grab the logbook and run. Behind you, the equipment sparks and hisses. By the time you reach your car, the red light on the tower goes dark.

"Be safe. Be loud. Be everything a mime can't be."
"Your friend, Gerald"

The radio comes on one final time. You're not surprised anymore. You're ready.
"You found the tower. You read the book. You know we're watching. Now let me tell you why."
The voice is tired. Not menacing. Tired.
"My name is Alan. I was an emergency dispatcher for 30 years. I took calls from people in trouble — car accidents, fires, crimes. I sent help. That was my job."
"When I retired, I couldn't stop. Couldn't stop listening. So I built the tower. I monitor the frequencies. I watch the roads. I track the people who live on them."
"Not to control. Not to harm. Just to make sure... if something happens... someone notices."
"You're not the only one I watch. There are dozens of you on this stretch. Drifters, gamblers, runners. I keep logs so if one of you disappears, I can tell someone where to start looking."
"You took my logbook. That's okay. I've got copies. But you should know — your page has more notes than most. You've survived things I've seen other people die from."
"I'm rooting for you. For what it's worth."
"I'm going to stop broadcasting to you now. You know I'm here. That's enough. If you ever need help — real help — tune to 87.14 and key the mic. I'll hear it."
The radio clicks off. The dial light fades.
You're alone on the road. But for the first time, you don't feel alone. Somewhere in a concrete bunker, an old dispatcher is watching the roads, making sure the lost don't stay lost.

Martinez wipes his eyes. Badge and all, this man is just a brother who lost a brother.

You hand over $1592. It's a lot. But his eyes looked real. The photograph looked real. The trembling was real — you know what fake desperation looks like, and this wasn't it.

This leaf, let's call it Gerald, flutters with an elegance borne of happenstance, a fragile vessel for the whims of the wind. Gerald's journey is not linear, nor is it bound by the rigid expectations of society's ceaselessly grinding gears. Instead, Gerald twirls, dips, and soars, embracing the chaos with a grace we can only aspire to. Now, consider the ant, industrious and steadfast, a creature of purpose and communal toil. Our ant, whom we'll name Beatrice, marches diligently on her quest for sustenance, her life a testament to the virtues of hard work and persistence. Yet, in the grand tapestry of the cosmos, what is Beatrice but a speck, a mere blip on the infinite canvas of the universe? And yet, does her insignificance in the face of the vast unknown render her efforts moot? I posit that it does not, for in the grand scheme, all actions, large and small, contribute to the intricate mosaic of existence. But back to Gerald, who by now has traversed the convoluted landscapes of our imagination, touching upon the existential questions that haunt the periphery of our consciousness. What can we learn from Gerald and Beatrice? Is it their resilience, their unyielding will to persist in the face of the insurmountable odds stacked against them by the very nature of their existence? Or is it, perhaps, the simple beauty of their dance, a reminder that life, in all its complexity and confusion, offers moments of sublime beauty, fleeting and precious, to be cherished and remembered? As Gerald finally comes to rest upon the earth, joining Beatrice in the eternal cycle of life and death, we are reminded that all journeys, whether they be of leaves or ants or the human heart, are interconnected in the grand, bewildering dance of the cosmos. So, what was the point of this story, you may ask? Well, that, my friend, is entirely up to you."

You wade into the ocean at night, the water black and endless. The waves push and pull at your body. Above you, the stars are scattered across the sky like spilled salt. Bioluminescence glows blue-green around your feet with each step.

A current catches you, stronger than you expected. The undertow pulls at your legs, dragging you away from shore. The beach lights grow smaller.

Do you fight the current directly, swim parallel to the beach, or relax and float?

You remember some old advice and swim sideways, parallel to the beach. Slowly, the current releases you, and you make your way back to shore.

Smart thinking. Fighting a riptide is how people drown.

What can we learn from Gerald and Beatrice? Is it their resilience, their unyielding will to persist in the face of the insurmountable odds stacked against them by the very nature of their existence? Or is it, perhaps, the simple beauty of their dance, a reminder that life, in all its complexity and confusion, offers moments of sublime beauty, fleeting and precious, to be cherished and remembered? As Gerald finally comes to rest upon the earth, joining Beatrice in the eternal cycle of life and death, we are reminded that all journeys, whether they be of leaves or ants or the human heart, are interconnected in the grand, bewildering dance of the cosmos. So, what was the point of this story, you may ask? Well, that, my friend, is entirely up to you."

══════════ ~ ~ ~ Morning, Day 160 ~ ~ ~ ══════════

You feel unstable.
You sit up in your car seat. Something feels... wrong.

You look in your rearview mirror and freeze.

There's a man in your back seat.

He's dressed in a vintage suit, the kind people wore in the 1920s. His face is pale. Too pale. And you can see right through him.

"Don't be alarmed," he says, his voice like static. "I just wanted to talk to a fellow gambler."

You are VERY alarmed.

"I lost everything at this casino, you know. 1928. Bet my house, my car, my wife's jewelry. Lost it all on one hand."

He laughs, but there's no humor in it.

"They found me the next morning in the river. I've been here ever since, watching others make the same mistakes."

You don't know what to say. What DO you say to a ghost?

"You're different, though. I can see it. You might actually make it out."

He starts to fade.

"Just remember: the house always wins. Unless you know when to walk away."

And then he's gone. You don't sleep the rest of the night.

You stop walking. The figure doesn't move. It's too far to make out any details, just a dark silhouette against the fading sky.

Do you approach the figure?You push through the grass toward the figure. As you get closer, you realize...

══════════ ~ ~ ~ Morning, Day 195 ~ ~ ~ ══════════
That was some quality sleep. Five stars. Would sleep again.

You wake up.
But something is wrong.
The world outside your car window is... gray. Not overcast. Not foggy. Just gray. Like someone forgot to color it in.
You try to move, but your body doesn't respond. You're frozen in place, staring at the gray nothing through your windshield.
And then you see it.
Yourself.
Standing outside the car. Staring back at you. Its face is yours, but the expression... the expression is something you've never made. Something you don't think a human face should be able to make.
"Did you really think you could keep going like this?"
The other you speaks, but its mouth doesn't move. The words just appear in your head, sharp and cold.
"Night after night. Hand after hand. Chasing a number that means nothing. Sleeping in a car. Eating whatever you can find. Talking to strangers who don't care if you live or die."
It steps closer to the car. Its movements are wrong. Jerky. Like a puppet with tangled strings.
"You've been breaking. Slowly. Piece by piece. And you didn't even notice, did you?"
The other you presses its face against the driver's side window. Up close, you can see that its eyes are wrong. The pupils are shaped like playing card suits. Spades. Hearts. Diamonds. Clubs. Rotating. Never stopping.
"I've been growing inside you. Every bad beat. Every sleepless night. Every time you told yourself 'just one more hand.' I grew. And grew. And grew."
It smiles. Your smile. But wider. Much, much wider.
"Now I'm strong enough to take over. Unless..."
The thing pauses. Its card-suit eyes spin faster.
"Unless you can prove you're still in there. Still human. Still sane."
Suddenly, you can move again. Your hands grip the steering wheel. Your foot finds the gas pedal.
"Answer me this, gambler. Answer me true. And maybe-MAYBE-I'll let you keep your mind."
The shadow asks you three questions. Think carefully.
"Why do you gamble?"
1. For the money.
2. For the thrill.
3. Because I can't stop.
4. I don't know anymore.

"Uncertainty. The most honest answer of all. You see yourself clearly now."
"What do you see when you look at the Dealer?"
1. An enemy.
2. A mirror.
3. Nothing. He's just a man doing a job.
4. Something that isn't human.

"Not human. Perhaps you're right. Perhaps you're projecting. The line between truth and delusion grows thin."
"If you could go back to the day you first walked into the casino with fifty dollars... would you walk away instead?"
1. Yes. I would walk away.
2. No. I regret nothing.
3. I don't know. I can't imagine any other life now.
4. There is no 'walking away.' This was always going to happen.

"Fatalism. You believe in destiny. That nothing could have changed this outcome. A comforting lie... or a terrible truth."
The shadow studies you. Its card-suit eyes slow their spinning.
"...No. There's nothing left. Just echoes. Just cards."
The shadow smiles wider. And wider. And wider still, until its face is nothing but teeth and darkness.
"Thank you for the body. I'll take good care of it."
It reaches through the window. Not breaking it. Just... phasing through, like the glass isn't there. Like nothing is there. Like reality is just a suggestion.
Cold fingers wrap around your throat. Your own fingers. Your own hands.
"Shhhhh. Don't fight it. You've been fighting for so long. Aren't you tired?"
You are tired. So tired. When was the last time you really slept? Really rested? Really felt at peace?
"Let go. I'll handle everything from here. The cards. The money. The endless nights. You don't have to carry it anymore."
The world goes dark. But it's not a scary dark. It's soft. Quiet. Like sinking into a warm bath.
You feel yourself drifting away. Becoming small. Smaller. A speck of consciousness in a vast empty space.
And then...
~ ~ ~ MADNESS ~ ~ ~
Your eyes open.
But they're not your eyes anymore.
You watch from somewhere deep inside as your body sits up. Stretches. Smiles a smile you've never smiled before.
"That's better."
Your voice. But not your words.
The thing wearing your skin looks around the car. At the pile of money. At the casino on the hill. At the endless road stretching into the distance.
"Now then. Where were we?"
It counts the money with fingers that used to be yours. It straightens the clothes on a body that used to be yours. It checks the rearview mirror with eyes that used to be yours.
And deep inside, in the tiny dark corner where you still exist, you scream.
But no one hears.
No one ever will.
The thing that used to be you gets out of the car. It walks toward the casino with a spring in its step. It's humming a tune that doesn't exist.
The Dealer looks up as it enters. For a moment-just a moment-his jade eye flickers with something like recognition. Like fear.
"...You."
The thing grins with your mouth.
"Miss me?"
It sits down at the table. It picks up the cards.
And somewhere inside, trapped forever in the prison of your own mind, you watch helplessly as the game continues.
Forever.
And ever.
And ever.
Your mind shattered.
Something else took the wheel.
And the Dealer... the Dealer remembers.
